diameter">Angular diameter</a></div></th><td>0.06&#8243; to 0.11&#8243;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-9"><a href="#cite_note-Pluto_Fact_Sheet-2">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Physorg_April_19,_2011_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Physorg_April_19,_2011-18">[11]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NH-Pluto-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5105" data-file-width="7489" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/NH-Pluto-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg/220px-NH-Pluto-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/NH-Pluto-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg/330px-NH-Pluto-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/NH-Pluto-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg/440px-NH-Pluto-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NH-Pluto-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mosaic of best-resolution images of Pluto from different angles</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Pluto</b> (<a href="/wiki/Minor_planet_designation" title="Minor planet designation">minor planet designation</a>: <b>134340 Pluto</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a>, a ring of <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">bodies beyond Neptune</a>. It was the first Kuiper belt object to be discovered and is the largest known <a href="/wiki/Plutoid" title="Plutoid">plutoid</a> (or <i>ice dwarf</i>).\n</p><p>Pluto was discovered by <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Tombaugh" title="Clyde Tombaugh">Clyde Tombaugh</a> in 1930 and was originally considered to be the <a href="/wiki/Planets_beyond_Neptune" title="Planets beyond Neptune">ninth planet</a> from the Sun. After 1992, its status as a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> was questioned following the discovery of several objects of similar size in the Kuiper belt. In 2005, <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, a dwarf planet in the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a> which is 27% more massive than Pluto, was discovered. This led the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU) to <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">define the term "planet"</a> formally in 2006, during their 26th General Assembly. That definition excluded Pluto and reclassified it as a dwarf planet.\n</p><p>Pluto is the largest and second-most-massive (after <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>) known dwarf planet in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, and the ninth-largest and tenth-most-massive known object directly orbiting the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. It is the largest known trans-Neptunian object by volume but is less massive than Eris. Like other Kuiper belt objects, Pluto is primarily made of ice and rock and is relatively small&#8212;about one-sixth the mass of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and one-third its volume. It has a moderately <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a> and inclined orbit during which it ranges from 30 to 49&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> or AU (4.4&#8211;7.4 billion&#160;km) from the Sun. This means that Pluto periodically comes closer to the Sun than <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, but a stable <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a> with Neptune prevents them from colliding.  Light from the Sun takes about 5.5 hours to reach Pluto at its average distance (39.5&#160;AU). \n</p><p>Pluto has <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Pluto" title="Moons of Pluto">five known moons</a>: <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a> (the largest, with a diameter just over half that of Pluto), <a href="/wiki/Styx_(moon)" title="Styx (moon)">Styx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">Nix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(moon)" title="Kerberos (moon)">Kerberos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">Hydra</a>. Pluto and Charon are sometimes considered a <a href="/wiki/Binary_system" title="Binary system">binary system</a> because the <a href="/wiki/Barycenter" title="Barycenter">barycenter</a> of their orbits does not lie within either body.\n</p><p>On July 14, 2015, the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> spacecraft became the first spacecraft to <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">fly by</a> Pluto. During its brief flyby, <i>New Horizons</i> made detailed measurements and observations of Pluto and its moons. In September 2016, astronomers announced that the reddish-brown cap of the north pole of Charon is composed of <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic</a> <a href="/wiki/Macromolecule" title="Macromolecule">macromolecules</a> that may be ingredients for the <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">emergence of life</a>, and produced from <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and other gases released from the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Pluto" title="Atmosphere of Pluto">atmosphere of Pluto</a> and transferred about 19,000&#160;km (12,000&#160;mi) to the orbiting moon.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Planets_beyond_Neptune" title="Planets beyond Neptune">Planets beyond Neptune</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_discovery_plates.png"><img alt="The same area of night sky with stars, shown twice, side by side. One of the bright points, located with an arrow, changes position between the two images." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="248" data-file-width="401" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c6/Pluto_discovery_plates.png/220px-Pluto_discovery_plates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c6/Pluto_discovery_plates.png/330px-Pluto_discovery_plates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/c/c6/Pluto_discovery_plates.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_discovery_plates.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Discovery photographs of Pluto</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clyde_W._Tombaugh.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="766" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Clyde_W._Tombaugh.jpeg/170px-Clyde_W._Tombaugh.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Clyde_W._Tombaugh.jpeg/255px-Clyde_W._Tombaugh.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Clyde_W._Tombaugh.jpeg/340px-Clyde_W._Tombaugh.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clyde_W._Tombaugh.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clyde Tombaugh, in Kansas</div></div></div>\n<p>In the 1840s, <a href="/wiki/Urbain_Le_Verrier" title="Urbain Le Verrier">Urbain Le Verrier</a> used <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">Newtonian mechanics</a> to predict the position of the then-undiscovered planet <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> after analyzing perturbations in the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[13]</a></sup> Subsequent observations of Neptune in the late 19th century led astronomers to speculate that Uranus\'s orbit was being disturbed by another planet besides Neptune.\n</p><p>In 1906, <a href="/wiki/Percival_Lowell" title="Percival Lowell">Percival Lowell</a>&#8212;a wealthy Bostonian who had founded <a href="/wiki/Lowell_Observatory" title="Lowell Observatory">Lowell Observatory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Flagstaff,_Arizona" title="Flagstaff, Arizona">Flagstaff, Arizona</a>, in 1894&#8212;started an extensive project in search of a possible ninth planet, which he termed "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planet_X" title="Planet X">Planet X</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tombaugh1946_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tombaugh1946-21">[14]</a></sup> By 1909, Lowell and <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Pickering" title="William Henry Pickering">William H. Pickering</a> had suggested several possible celestial coordinates for such a planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyt_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyt-22">[15]</a></sup> Lowell and his observatory conducted his search until his death in 1916, but to no avail. Unknown to Lowell, his surveys had captured two faint images of Pluto on March 19 and April 7, 1915, but they were not recognized for what they were.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyt_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyt-22">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Littman1990_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Littman1990-23">[16]</a></sup> There are fourteen other known <a href="/wiki/Precovery" title="Precovery">precovery</a> observations, with the earliest made by the <a href="/wiki/Yerkes_Observatory" title="Yerkes Observatory">Yerkes Observatory</a> on August 20, 1909.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuchwaldDimarioWild2000_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-BuchwaldDimarioWild2000-24">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Percival\'s widow, Constance Lowell, entered into a ten-year legal battle with the Lowell Observatory over her husband\'s legacy, and the search for Planet X did not resume until 1929.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroswell199750_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroswell199750-25">[18]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesto_Melvin_Slipher" title="Vesto Melvin Slipher">Vesto Melvin Slipher</a>, the observatory director, gave the job of locating Planet X to 23-year-old <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Tombaugh" title="Clyde Tombaugh">Clyde Tombaugh</a>, who had just arrived at the observatory after Slipher had been impressed by a sample of his astronomical drawings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroswell199750_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroswell199750-25">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tombaugh\'s task was to systematically image the night sky in pairs of photographs, then examine each pair and determine whether any objects had shifted position. Using a <a href="/wiki/Blink_comparator" title="Blink comparator">blink comparator</a>, he rapidly shifted back and forth between views of each of the plates to create the illusion of movement of any objects that had changed position or appearance between photographs. On February 18, 1930, after nearly a year of searching, Tombaugh discovered a possible moving object on photographic plates taken on January 23 and 29. A lesser-quality photograph taken on January 21 helped confirm the movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroswell199752_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroswell199752-26">[19]</a></sup> After the observatory obtained further confirmatory photographs, news of the discovery was telegraphed to the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College_Observatory" title="Harvard College Observatory">Harvard College Observatory</a> on March 13, 1930.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyt_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyt-22">[15]</a></sup> Pluto has yet to complete a full orbit of the Sun since its discovery because one Plutonian year is 247.68 years long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h3>\n<p>The discovery made headlines around the globe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[21]</a></sup> Lowell Observatory, which had the right to name the new object, received more than 1,000 suggestions from all over the world, ranging from Atlas to Zymal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pluto_guide_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-pluto_guide-29">[22]</a></sup> Tombaugh urged Slipher to suggest a name for the new object quickly before someone else did.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pluto_guide_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-pluto_guide-29">[22]</a></sup> Constance Lowell proposed <i><a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a></i>, then <i>Percival</i> and finally <i>Constance</i>. These suggestions were disregarded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mager_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mager-30">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The name Pluto, after the <a href="/wiki/Pluto_(mythology)" title="Pluto (mythology)">god of the underworld</a>, was proposed by <a href="/wiki/Venetia_Burney" title="Venetia Burney">Venetia Burney</a> (1918&#8211;2009), an eleven-year-old schoolgirl in <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>, England, who was interested in <a href="/wiki/Classical_mythology" title="Classical mythology">classical mythology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venetia_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Venetia-31">[24]</a></sup> She suggested it in a conversation with her grandfather <a href="/wiki/Falconer_Madan" title="Falconer Madan">Falconer Madan</a>, a former librarian at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Bodleian_Library" title="Bodleian Library">Bodleian Library</a>, who passed the name to astronomy professor <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hall_Turner" title="Herbert Hall Turner">Herbert Hall Turner</a>, who cabled it to colleagues in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venetia_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Venetia-31">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Each member of the Lowell Observatory was allowed to vote on a short-list of three potential names: <a href="/wiki/Minerva" title="Minerva">Minerva</a> (which was already the name for an asteroid), <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Cronus</a> (which had lost reputation through being proposed by the unpopular astronomer <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Jackson_See" title="Thomas Jefferson Jackson See">Thomas Jefferson Jackson See</a>), and Pluto. Pluto received every vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroswell199754&#8211;55_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroswell199754&#8211;55-32">[25]</a></sup> The name was announced on May 1, 1930.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venetia_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Venetia-31">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[26]</a></sup> Upon the announcement, Madan gave Venetia &#163;5 (equivalent to 300 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GBP" title="GBP">GBP</a>, or 450 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USD" title="USD">USD</a> in 2014)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-UK_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-UK-34">[27]</a></sup> as a reward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venetia_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-Venetia-31">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The final choice of name was helped in part by the fact that the first two letters of <i>Pluto</i> are the initials of Percival Lowell. Pluto\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_symbol" title="Astronomical symbol">astronomical symbol</a> <span class="nowrap">(<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_symbol.svg"><img alt="Pluto symbol.svg" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Pluto_symbol.svg/20px-Pluto_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Pluto_symbol.svg/30px-Pluto_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Pluto_symbol.svg/40px-Pluto_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a>,</span> <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> U+2647, &#9799;) was then created as a <a href="/wiki/Monogram" title="Monogram">monogram</a> constructed from the letters "PL".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPL/NASA_Pluto\'s_Symbol_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPL/NASA_Pluto\'s_Symbol-35">[28]</a></sup> Pluto\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrological_symbol" title="Astrological symbol">astrological symbol</a> resembles that of Neptune <span class="nowrap">(<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptune_symbol.svg"><img alt="Neptune symbol.svg" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Neptune_symbol.svg/20px-Neptune_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Neptune_symbol.svg/30px-Neptune_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Neptune_symbol.svg/40px-Neptune_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a>)</span>, but has a circle in place of the middle prong of the trident <span class="nowrap">(<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto%27s_astrological_symbol.svg"><img alt="Pluto\'s astrological symbol.svg" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Pluto%27s_astrological_symbol.svg/20px-Pluto%27s_astrological_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Pluto%27s_astrological_symbol.svg/30px-Pluto%27s_astrological_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Pluto%27s_astrological_symbol.svg/40px-Pluto%27s_astrological_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a>)</span>.\n</p><p>The name was soon embraced by wider culture. In 1930, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a> was apparently inspired by it when he introduced for <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse" title="Mickey Mouse">Mickey Mouse</a> a canine companion named <a href="/wiki/Pluto_(Disney)" title="Pluto (Disney)">Pluto</a>, although <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">Disney</a> animator <a href="/wiki/Ben_Sharpsteen" title="Ben Sharpsteen">Ben Sharpsteen</a> could not confirm why the name was given.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heinrichs2006_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heinrichs2006-36">[29]</a></sup> In 1941, <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Glenn T. Seaborg</a> named the newly created <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> after Pluto, in keeping with the tradition of naming elements after newly discovered planets, following <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, which was named after Uranus, and <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a>, which was named after Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClarkHobart2000_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-ClarkHobart2000-37">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most languages use the name "Pluto" in various transliterations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[h]</a></sup> In Japanese, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Houei_Nojiri" title="Houei Nojiri">Houei Nojiri</a> suggested the translation <i>Mei&#333;sei</i> <span style="font-weight:normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">&#20901;&#29579;&#26143;</span>, "Star of the King (God) of the Underworld")</span>, and this was borrowed into Chinese, Korean, and Vietnamese (which instead uses "Sao Di&#234;m V&#432;&#417;ng", which was derived from the Chinese term &#38331;&#29579; (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yama_(East_Asia)" title="Yama (East Asia)">Y&#225;nw&#225;ng</a>), as "minh" is a homophone for the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Vietnamese_vocabulary" title="Sino-Vietnamese vocabulary">Sino-Vietnamese</a> words for "dark" (&#20901;) and "bright" (&#26126;)).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RenshawIhara2000_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-RenshawIhara2000-39">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nineplan_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-nineplan-40">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bathrobe_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bathrobe-41">[33]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_India" title="Languages of India">Indian languages</a> use the name Pluto, but others, such as <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>, use the name of <i><a href="/wiki/Yama" title="Yama">Yama</a></i>, the God of Death in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yama_(East_Asia)" title="Yama (East Asia)">Buddhist</a> mythology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nineplan_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-nineplan-40">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_languages" title="Polynesian languages">Polynesian languages</a> also tend to use the indigenous god of the underworld, as in <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="M&#257;ori language">M&#257;ori</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Whiro" title="Whiro">Whiro</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nineplan_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-nineplan-40">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planet_X_disproved">Planet X disproved</span></h3>\n<p>Once Pluto was found, its faintness and lack of a resolvable disc cast doubt on the idea that it was Lowell\'s <a href="/wiki/Planets_beyond_Neptune" title="Planets beyond Neptune">Planet X</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tombaugh1946_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tombaugh1946-21">[14]</a></sup> Estimates of Pluto\'s mass were revised downward throughout the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[34]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders" style="float: right; margin-right: 0; margin-left: 1em; text-align: center;">\n<caption>Mass estimates for Pluto\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th scope="col">Year\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Mass\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Estimate by\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">1915\n</th>\n<td>7 Earth\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Percival_Lowell" title="Percival Lowell">Lowell</a> (prediction for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planet_X" title="Planet X">Planet X</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tombaugh1946_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tombaugh1946-21">[14]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">1931\n</th>\n<td>1 Earth\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Seth_Barnes_Nicholson" title="Seth Barnes Nicholson">Nicholson</a> &amp; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicholas_U._Mayall" title="Nicholas U. Mayall">Mayall</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAS1931.91_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-RAS1931.91-43">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholson_et_al_1930_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholson_et_al_1930-44">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholson_et_al_1931_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholson_et_al_1931-45">[37]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">1948\n</th>\n<td>0.1 (1/10) Earth\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gerard_Kuiper" title="Gerard Kuiper">Kuiper</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuiper_10.1086/126255_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuiper_10.1086/126255-46">[38]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">1976\n</th>\n<td>0.01 (1/100) Earth\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Dale_Cruikshank" title="Dale Cruikshank">Cruikshank</a>, Pilcher, &amp; <a href="/wiki/David_Morrison_(astrophysicist)" title="David Morrison (astrophysicist)">Morrison</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroswell199757_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroswell199757-47">[39]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">1978\n</th>\n<td>0.0015 (1/650) Earth\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/James_W._Christy" title="James W. Christy">Christy</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Robert_Sutton_Harrington" title="Robert Sutton Harrington">Harrington</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChristyHarrington1978_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChristyHarrington1978-48">[40]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">2006\n</th>\n<td>0.00218 (1/459) Earth\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marc_W._Buie" title="Marc W. Buie">Buie</a> et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuieGrundyYoung_2006_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-BuieGrundyYoung_2006-49">[41]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Astronomers initially calculated its mass based on its presumed effect on Neptune and Uranus. In 1931, Pluto was calculated to be roughly the mass of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, with further calculations in 1948 bringing the mass down to roughly that of <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholson_et_al_1930_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholson_et_al_1930-44">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuiper_10.1086/126255_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kuiper_10.1086/126255-46">[38]</a></sup> In 1976, Dale Cruikshank, Carl Pilcher and David Morrison of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii" title="University of Hawaii">University of Hawaii</a> calculated Pluto\'s <a href="/wiki/Albedo#Astronomical_albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> for the first time, finding that it matched that for methane ice; this meant Pluto had to be exceptionally luminous for its size and therefore could not be more than 1 percent the mass of Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroswell199757_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroswell199757-47">[39]</a></sup> (Pluto\'s albedo is <span class="nowrap">1.4&#8211;1.9</span> times that of Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-10"><a href="#cite_note-Pluto_Fact_Sheet-2">[1]</a></sup>)\n</p><p>In 1978, the discovery of Pluto\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a> allowed the measurement of Pluto\'s mass for the first time: roughly 0.2% that of Earth, and far too small to account for the discrepancies in the orbit of Uranus. Subsequent searches for an alternative Planet X, notably by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Sutton_Harrington" title="Robert Sutton Harrington">Robert Sutton Harrington</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SeidelmannHarrington1988_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-SeidelmannHarrington1988-50">[42]</a></sup> failed. In 1992, <a href="/wiki/E._Myles_Standish" title="E. Myles Standish">Myles Standish</a> used data from <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a>\'</i>s flyby of Neptune in 1989, which had revised the estimates of Neptune\'s mass downward by 0.5%&#8212;an amount comparable to the mass of Mars&#8212;to recalculate its gravitational effect on Uranus. With the new figures added in, the discrepancies, and with them the need for a Planet X, vanished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Standish1993_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Standish1993-51">[43]</a></sup> Today, the majority of scientists agree that Planet X, as Lowell defined it, does not exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Standage2000_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Standage2000-52">[44]</a></sup> Lowell had made a prediction of Planet X\'s orbit and position in 1915 that was fairly close to Pluto\'s actual orbit and its position at that time; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ernest_W._Brown" title="Ernest W. Brown">Ernest W. Brown</a> concluded soon after Pluto\'s discovery that this was a coincidence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tenn1994_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tenn1994-53">[45]</a></sup> a view still held today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Standish1993_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Standish1993-51">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Definition_of_planet" title="Definition of planet">Definition of planet</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><div class="noresize"><map name="ImageMap_1_1035046547"><area alt="Earth" alt="Sedna" coords="145,143,9" href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" shape="circle" title="Sedna"/><area alt="Sedna" coords="130,166,160,173" href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" shape="rect" title="Sedna"/><area alt="2002 MS4" coords="192,143,8" href="/wiki/(307261)_2002_MS4" shape="circle" title="2002 MS4"/><area alt="2002 MS4" coords="171,165,210,172" href="/wiki/(307261)_2002_MS4" shape="rect" title="2002 MS4"/><area alt="Vanth" coords="223,136,5" href="/wiki/90482_Orcus#Satellite_Vanth" shape="circle" title="Vanth"/><area alt="Vanth" coords="202,122,219,128" href="/wiki/90482_Orcus#Satellite_Vanth" shape="rect" title="Vanth"/><area alt="Orcus" coords="237,143,8" href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" shape="circle" title="Orcus"/><area alt="Orcus" coords="224,165,250,173" href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" shape="rect" title="Orcus"/><area alt="Actaea" coords="288,132,3" href="/wiki/Actaea_(moon)" shape="circle" title="Actaea"/><area alt="Actaea" coords="267,118,289,125" href="/wiki/Actaea_(moon)" shape="rect" title="Actaea"/><area alt="Salacia" coords="277,143,8" href="/wiki/120347_Salacia" shape="circle" title="Salacia"/><area alt="Salacia" coords="261,165,294,172" href="/wiki/120347_Salacia" shape="rect" title="Salacia"/><area alt="File:EightTNOs.png" coords="0,0,310,225" href="/wiki/File:EightTNOs.png" shape="rect" title="File:EightTNOs.png"/></map><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1995" data-file-width="2750" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/EightTNOs.png/310px-EightTNOs.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/EightTNOs.png/465px-EightTNOs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/EightTNOs.png/620px-EightTNOs.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_1_1035046547" width="310"/></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EightTNOs.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artistic comparison of <a class="mw-selflink selflink">Pluto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(225088)_2007_OR10" title="(225088) 2007 OR10">2007 OR<sub>10</sub></a></span>, <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>, <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(307261)_2002_MS4" title="(307261) 2002 MS4">2002 MS<sub>4</sub></a></span>, <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>, <a href="/wiki/120347_Salacia" title="120347 Salacia">Salacia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> along with the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini" style="float:bottom"><span style="margin-right:-0.125em">[ </span><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:TNO_imagemap" title="Template:TNO imagemap"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:TNO_imagemap" title="Template talk:TNO imagemap"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:TNO_imagemap&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul><span style="margin-left:-0.125em"> ]</span></div></div></div></div>\n<p>From 1992 onward, many bodies were discovered orbiting in the same volume as Pluto, showing that Pluto is part of a population of objects called the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a>. This made its official status as a planet controversial, with many questioning whether Pluto should be considered together with or separately from its surrounding population. Museum and planetarium directors occasionally created controversy by omitting Pluto from planetary models of the Solar System. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hayden_Planetarium" title="Hayden Planetarium">Hayden Planetarium</a> reopened&#8212;in February 2000, after renovation&#8212;with a model of only eight planets, which made headlines almost a year later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyson2001_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tyson2001-54">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As objects increasingly closer in size to Pluto were discovered in the region, it was argued that Pluto should be reclassified as one of the Kuiper belt objects, just as <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>, <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">Pallas</a>, <a href="/wiki/3_Juno" title="3 Juno">Juno</a> and <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a> lost their planet status after the discovery of many other <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>. On July 29, 2005, astronomers at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caltech" title="Caltech">Caltech</a> announced the discovery of a new <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian object</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, which was substantially more massive than Pluto and the most massive object discovered in the Solar System since <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> in 1846. Its discoverers and the press initially called it the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenth_planet" title="Tenth planet">tenth planet</a>, although there was no official consensus at the time on whether to call it a planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-JPL_press_release_07-29-2005_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-JPL_press_release_07-29-2005-55">[47]</a></sup> Others in the astronomical community considered the discovery the strongest argument for reclassifying Pluto as a minor planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="IAU_classification">IAU classification</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">IAU definition of planet</a></div>\n<p>The debate came to a head in August 2006, with an <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">IAU resolution</a> that created an official definition for the term "planet". According to this resolution, there are three conditions for an object in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> to be considered a planet:\n</p>\n<ol><li>The object must be in orbit around the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>.</li>\n<li>The object must be massive enough to be rounded by its own gravity. More specifically, its own gravity should pull it into a shape defined by <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a>.</li>\n<li>It must have <a href="/wiki/Clearing_the_neighbourhood" title="Clearing the neighbourhood">cleared the neighborhood</a> around its orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU2006_GA26-5-6_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU2006_GA26-5-6-57">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU0603_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU0603-58">[50]</a></sup></li></ol>\n<p>Pluto fails to meet the third condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Margot2015_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Margot2015-59">[51]</a></sup> Its mass is substantially less than the combined mass of the other objects in its orbit: 0.07 times, in contrast to Earth, which is 1.7 million times the remaining mass in its orbit (excluding the moon).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-what_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-what-60">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU0603_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-IAU0603-58">[50]</a></sup> The IAU further decided that bodies that, like Pluto, meet criteria 1 and 2, but do not meet criterion 3 would be called <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a>. In September 2006, the IAU included Pluto, and Eris and its moon <a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" title="Dysnomia (moon)">Dysnomia</a>, in their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Catalogue" title="Minor Planet Catalogue">Minor Planet Catalogue</a>, giving them the official <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet_designation" title="Minor planet designation">minor planet designations</a> "(134340) Pluto", "(136199) Eris", and "(136199) Eris I Dysnomia".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC_8747_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAUC_8747-61">[53]</a></sup> Had Pluto been included upon its discovery in 1930, it would have likely been designated 1164, following <a href="/wiki/1163_Saga" title="1163 Saga">1163 Saga</a>, which was discovered a month earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There has been some resistance within the astronomical community toward the reclassification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geoff2006c_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-geoff2006c-63">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruibal-1999_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruibal-1999-64">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britt-2006_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britt-2006-65">[57]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alan_Stern" title="Alan Stern">Alan Stern</a>, principal investigator with <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>\'s <i>New Horizons</i> mission to Pluto, derided the IAU resolution, stating that "the definition stinks, for technical reasons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geoff2006a_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-geoff2006a-66">[58]</a></sup> Stern contended that, by the terms of the new definition, Earth, Mars, Jupiter, and Neptune, all of which share their orbits with asteroids, would be excluded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newscientistspace_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-newscientistspace-67">[59]</a></sup> He argued that all big spherical moons, including the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, should likewise be considered planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-News.discovery.com_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-News.discovery.com-68">[60]</a></sup> He also stated that because less than five percent of astronomers voted for it, the decision was not representative of the entire astronomical community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newscientistspace_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-newscientistspace-67">[59]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marc_W._Buie" title="Marc W. Buie">Marc W. Buie</a>, then at the Lowell Observatory petitioned against the definition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie2006_IAU_response_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buie2006_IAU_response-69">[61]</a></sup> Others have supported the IAU. <a href="/wiki/Michael_E._Brown" title="Michael E. Brown">Mike Brown</a>, the astronomer who discovered Eris, said "through this whole crazy circus-like procedure, somehow the right answer was stumbled on. It\'s been a long time coming. Science is self-correcting eventually, even when strong emotions are involved."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overbye2006_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Overbye2006-70">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Public reception to the IAU decision was mixed. Many accepted the reclassification, but some sought to overturn the decision with online petitions urging the IAU to consider reinstatement. A resolution introduced by some members of the <a href="/wiki/California_State_Assembly" title="California State Assembly">California State Assembly</a> facetiously called the IAU decision a "scientific heresy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeVore2006_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeVore2006-71">[63]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico_House_of_Representatives" title="New Mexico House of Representatives">New Mexico House of Representatives</a> passed a resolution in honor of Tombaugh, a longtime resident of that state, that declared that Pluto will always be considered a planet while in New Mexican skies and that March 13, 2007, was Pluto Planet Day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holden2007_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holden2007-72">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gutierrez2007_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gutierrez2007-73">[65]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Senate" title="Illinois Senate">Illinois Senate</a> passed a similar resolution in 2009, on the basis that Clyde Tombaugh, the discoverer of Pluto, was born in Illinois. The resolution asserted that Pluto was "unfairly downgraded to a \'dwarf\' planet" by the IAU."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ILGA_SR0046_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-ILGA_SR0046-74">[66]</a></sup> Some members of the public have also rejected the change, citing the disagreement within the scientific community on the issue, or for sentimental reasons, maintaining that they have always known Pluto as a planet and will continue to do so regardless of the IAU decision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sapa-AP_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sapa-AP-75">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2006, in its 17th annual words-of-the-year vote, the <a href="/wiki/American_Dialect_Society" title="American Dialect Society">American Dialect Society</a> voted <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plutoed" title="Plutoed">plutoed</a></i> as the word of the year. To "pluto" is to "demote or devalue someone or something".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msnbc_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-msnbc-76">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Researchers on both sides of the debate gathered in August 2008, at the Johns Hopkins University <a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">Applied Physics Laboratory</a> for a conference that included back-to-back talks on the current IAU definition of a planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minkel2008_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Minkel2008-77">[69]</a></sup> Entitled "The Great Planet Debate",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Great_Planet_Debate_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Great_Planet_Debate-78">[70]</a></sup> the conference published a post-conference press release indicating that scientists could not come to a consensus about the definition of planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PSIedu_press_release_2008-09-19_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-PSIedu_press_release_2008-09-19-79">[71]</a></sup> In June 2008, the IAU had announced in a press release that the term "<a href="/wiki/Plutoid" title="Plutoid">plutoid</a>" would henceforth be used to refer to Pluto and other objects that have an orbital <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axis</a> greater than that of Neptune and enough mass to be of near-spherical shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU0804_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU0804-80">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discover_2009-JANp76_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Discover_2009-JANp76-81">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_News,_July_5,_2008_p._7_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_News,_July_5,_2008_p._7-82">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit">Orbit</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:auto; max-width:908px;">\n<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_skypath_1900-2050.png" title="Pluto was discovered in 1930 near the star &#948; Geminorum, and merely coincidentally crossing the ecliptic at this time of discovery. Pluto moves about 7 degrees east per decade with small apparent retrograde motion as seen from Earth. Pluto was closer to the Sun than Neptune between 1979 and 1999."><img alt="" data-file-height="491" data-file-width="4445" decoding="async" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pluto_skypath_1900-2050.png/900px-Pluto_skypath_1900-2050.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pluto_skypath_1900-2050.png/1350px-Pluto_skypath_1900-2050.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pluto_skypath_1900-2050.png/1800px-Pluto_skypath_1900-2050.png 2x" width="900"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pluto_skypath_1900-2050.png" title="File:Pluto skypath 1900-2050.png"> </a></div>Pluto was discovered in 1930 near the star <a href="/wiki/Delta_Geminorum" title="Delta Geminorum">&#948; Geminorum</a>, and merely coincidentally crossing the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a> at this time of discovery. Pluto moves about 7 degrees east per decade with small <a href="/wiki/Apparent_retrograde_motion" title="Apparent retrograde motion">apparent retrograde motion</a> as seen from Earth. Pluto was closer to the Sun than Neptune between 1979 and 1999.</div>\n</div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Pluto_orbit.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Animation_of_Pluto_orbit.gif/220px-Animation_of_Pluto_orbit.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Animation_of_Pluto_orbit.gif/330px-Animation_of_Pluto_orbit.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Animation_of_Pluto_orbit.gif/440px-Animation_of_Pluto_orbit.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Pluto_orbit.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of Pluto<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> orbit from 1900 to 2100<br/><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Yellow; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160; Sun</span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Gold ; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160; Saturn</span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Cyan ; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160; Uranus</span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color: RoyalBlue ; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160; Neptune</span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160; Pluto</span></div></div></div>\n<p>Pluto\'s orbital period is currently about 248 years. Its orbital characteristics are substantially different from those of the planets, which follow nearly circular orbits around the Sun close to a flat reference <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plane_(mathematics)" title="Plane (mathematics)">plane</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>. In contrast, Pluto\'s orbit is moderately <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">inclined</a> relative to the ecliptic (over 17&#176;) and moderately <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elliptical" title="Elliptical">elliptical</a>). This eccentricity means a small region of Pluto\'s orbit lies closer to the Sun than Neptune\'s. The Pluto&#8211;Charon barycenter came to <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">perihelion</a> on September 5, 1989,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl-ssd-horizons_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-jpl-ssd-horizons-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perihelion_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perihelion-83">[i]</a></sup> and was last closer to the Sun than Neptune between February 7, 1979, and February 11, 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pluto990209_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-pluto990209-84">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the long term, Pluto\'s orbit is <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">chaotic</a>. Computer simulations can be used to predict its position for several million years (both <a href="/wiki/Time_reversibility" title="Time reversibility">forward and backward</a> in time), but after intervals longer than the <a href="/wiki/Lyapunov_time" title="Lyapunov time">Lyapunov time</a> of 10&#8211;20 million years, calculations become speculative: Pluto is sensitive to immeasurably small details of the Solar System, hard-to-predict factors that will gradually change Pluto\'s position in its orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sussman88_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-sussman88-85">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wisdom91_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-wisdom91-86">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axis</a> of Pluto\'s orbit varies between about 39.3 and 39.6&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">au</a> with a period of about 19,951 years, corresponding to an orbital period varying between 246 and 249 years. The semi-major axis and period are presently getting longer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williams71_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-williams71-87">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:229px;max-width:229px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plutoorbit1.5sideview.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="247" data-file-width="249" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Plutoorbit1.5sideview.gif/225px-Plutoorbit1.5sideview.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0a/Plutoorbit1.5sideview.gif 1.5x" width="225"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Orbit of Pluto&#160;&#8211; ecliptic view. This "side view" of Pluto\'s orbit (in red) shows its large inclination to the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheKuiperBelt_Orbits_Pluto_Polar.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/TheKuiperBelt_Orbits_Pluto_Polar.svg/225px-TheKuiperBelt_Orbits_Pluto_Polar.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/TheKuiperBelt_Orbits_Pluto_Polar.svg/338px-TheKuiperBelt_Orbits_Pluto_Polar.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/TheKuiperBelt_Orbits_Pluto_Polar.svg/450px-TheKuiperBelt_Orbits_Pluto_Polar.svg.png 2x" width="225"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Orbit of Pluto&#160;&#8211; polar view. This "view from above" shows how Pluto\'s orbit (in red) is less circular than Neptune\'s (in blue), and how Pluto is sometimes closer to the Sun than Neptune. The darker sections of both orbits show where they pass below the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plane_of_the_ecliptic" title="Plane of the ecliptic">plane of the ecliptic</a>.</div></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_Neptune">Relationship with Neptune</span></h3>\n<p>Despite Pluto\'s orbit appearing to cross that of Neptune when viewed from directly above, the two objects\' orbits are aligned so that they can never collide or even approach closely.\n</p><p>The two orbits do not intersect. When Pluto is closest to the Sun, and hence closest to Neptune\'s orbit as viewed from above, it is also the farthest above Neptune\'s path. Pluto\'s orbit passes about 8 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> above that of Neptune, preventing a collision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huainn01_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-huainn01-88">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunter2004_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hunter2004-89">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malhotra-9planets_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-malhotra-9planets-90">[81]</a></sup>\n</p><p>This alone is not enough to protect Pluto; <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbations</a> from the planets (especially Neptune) could alter Pluto\'s orbit (such as its <a href="/wiki/Apsidal_precession" title="Apsidal precession">orbital precession</a>) over millions of years so that a collision could be possible. However, Pluto is also protected by its 2:3 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a> with <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>: for every two orbits that Pluto makes around the Sun, Neptune makes three. Each cycle lasts about 495 years. This pattern is such that, in each 495-year cycle, the first time Pluto is near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a>, Neptune is over 50&#176; behind Pluto. By Pluto\'s second perihelion, Neptune will have completed a further one and a half of its own orbits, and so will be nearly 130&#176; ahead of Pluto. Pluto and Neptune\'s minimum separation is over 17&#160;AU, which is greater than Pluto\'s minimum separation from Uranus (11&#160;AU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malhotra-9planets_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-malhotra-9planets-90">[81]</a></sup> The minimum separation between Pluto and Neptune actually occurs near the time of Pluto\'s aphelion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williams71_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-williams71-87">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 2:3 resonance between the two bodies is highly stable, and has been preserved over millions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sp-345_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-sp-345-91">[82]</a></sup> This prevents their orbits from changing relative to one another, and so the two bodies can never pass near each other. Even if Pluto\'s orbit were not inclined, the two bodies could never collide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malhotra-9planets_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-malhotra-9planets-90">[81]</a></sup> The long term stability of the mean-motion resonance is due to phase protection. If Pluto\'s period is slightly shorter than 3/2 of Neptune, its orbit relative to Neptune will drift, causing it to make closer approaches behind Neptune\'s orbit. The strong gravitational pull between the two causes <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a> to be transferred to Pluto, at Neptune\'s expense. This moves Pluto into a slightly larger orbit, where it travels slightly more slowly, according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_third_law" title="Kepler\'s third law">Kepler\'s third law</a>. After many such repetitions, Pluto is sufficiently slowed, and Neptune sufficiently sped up, that Pluto orbit relative to Neptune drifts in the opposite direction until the process is reversed. The whole process takes about 20,000 years to complete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malhotra-9planets_90-3"><a href="#cite_note-malhotra-9planets-90">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sp-345_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-sp-345-91">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen_Hubbard_1965_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen_Hubbard_1965-92">[83]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_factors">Other factors</span></h4>\n<p>Numerical studies have shown that over millions of years, the general nature of the alignment between the orbits of Pluto and Neptune does not change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huainn01_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-huainn01-88">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williams71_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-williams71-87">[78]</a></sup> There are several other resonances and interactions that enhance Pluto\'s stability. These arise principally from two additional mechanisms (besides the 2:3 mean-motion resonance).\n</p><p>First, Pluto\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argument_of_perihelion" title="Argument of perihelion">argument of perihelion</a>, the angle between the point where it crosses the ecliptic and the point where it is closest to the Sun, <a href="/wiki/Libration" title="Libration">librates</a> around 90&#176;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williams71_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-williams71-87">[78]</a></sup> This means that when Pluto is closest to the Sun, it is at its farthest above the plane of the Solar System, preventing encounters with Neptune. This is a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Kozai_mechanism" title="Kozai mechanism">Kozai mechanism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huainn01_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-huainn01-88">[79]</a></sup> which relates the eccentricity of an orbit to its inclination to a larger perturbing body&#8212;in this case Neptune. Relative to Neptune, the amplitude of libration is 38&#176;, and so the angular separation of Pluto\'s perihelion to the orbit of Neptune is always greater than 52&#176; <span class="nowrap">(90&#176;&#8211;38&#176;)</span>. The closest such angular separation occurs every 10,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sp-345_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-sp-345-91">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Second, the longitudes of ascending nodes of the two bodies&#8212;the points where they cross the ecliptic&#8212;are in near-resonance with the above libration. When the two longitudes are the same&#8212;that is, when one could draw a straight line through both nodes and the Sun&#8212;Pluto\'s perihelion lies exactly at 90&#176;, and hence it comes closest to the Sun when it is highest above Neptune\'s orbit. This is known as the <i>1:1 superresonance</i>. All the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jovian_planets" title="Jovian planets">Jovian planets</a>, particularly Jupiter, play a role in the creation of the superresonance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huainn01_88-3"><a href="#cite_note-huainn01-88">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quasi-satellite">Quasi-satellite</span></h3>\n<p>In 2012, it was hypothesized that <a href="/wiki/15810_Arawn" title="15810 Arawn">15810 Arawn</a> could be a <a href="/wiki/Quasi-satellite" title="Quasi-satellite">quasi-satellite</a> of Pluto, a specific type of co-orbital configuration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quasi_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-quasi-93">[84]</a></sup> According to the hypothesis, the object would be a quasi-satellite of Pluto for about 350,000 years out of every two-million-year period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quasi_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-quasi-93">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S&amp;T_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-S&amp;T-94">[85]</a></sup> Measurements made by the <i>New Horizons</i> spacecraft in 2015 made it possible to calculate the orbit of Arawn more accurately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2016maynasa_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-2016maynasa-95">[86]</a></sup> These calculations confirm the overall dynamics described in the hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-analemma_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-analemma-96">[87]</a></sup> However, it is not agreed upon among astronomers whether Arawn should be classified as a quasi-satellite of Pluto based on this motion, since its orbit is primarily controlled by Neptune with only occasional smaller perturbations caused by Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter_et_al_2016_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-porter_et_al_2016-97">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2016maynasa_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-2016maynasa-95">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-analemma_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-analemma-96">[87]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotation">Rotation</span></h2>\n<p>Pluto\'s <a href="/wiki/Rotation_period" title="Rotation period">rotation period</a>, its day, is equal to 6.39 <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-axis_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-axis-98">[89]</a></sup> Like <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, Pluto rotates on its "side" in its orbital plane, with an axial tilt of 120&#176;, and so its seasonal variation is extreme; at its <a href="/wiki/Solstice" title="Solstice">solstices</a>, one-fourth of its surface is in continuous daylight, whereas another fourth is in continuous darkness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oregon_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-oregon-99">[90]</a></sup> The reason for this unusual orientation has been debated. Research from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Arizona" title="University of Arizona">University of Arizona</a> has suggested that it may be due to the way that a body\'s spin will always adjust to minimise energy. This could mean a body reorienting itself to put extraneous mass near the equator and regions lacking mass tend towards the poles. This is called <i><a href="/wiki/Polar_wander" title="Polar wander">polar wander</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[91]</a></sup> According to a paper released from the University of Arizona, this could be caused by masses of frozen nitrogen building up in shadowed areas of the dwarf planet. These masses would cause the body to reorient itself, leading to its unusual axial tilt of 120&#176;. The buildup of nitrogen is due to Pluto\'s vast distance from the Sun. At the equator, temperatures can drop to &#8722;240&#160;&#176;C (&#8722;400.0&#160;&#176;F; 33.1&#160;K), causing nitrogen to freeze as water would freeze on Earth. The same effect seen on Pluto would be observed on Earth if the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheet</a> was several times larger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[92]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto-01_Stern_03_Pluto_Color_TXT.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8000" data-file-width="8000" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Pluto-01_Stern_03_Pluto_Color_TXT.jpg/220px-Pluto-01_Stern_03_Pluto_Color_TXT.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Pluto-01_Stern_03_Pluto_Color_TXT.jpg/330px-Pluto-01_Stern_03_Pluto_Color_TXT.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Pluto-01_Stern_03_Pluto_Color_TXT.jpg/440px-Pluto-01_Stern_03_Pluto_Color_TXT.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pluto-01_Stern_03_Pluto_Color_TXT.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>High-resolution <a href="/wiki/New_Horizons#Ralph_telescope" title="New Horizons">MVIC</a> image of Pluto in enhanced color to bring out differences in surface composition</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NH-Pluto-WaterIceDetected-BlueRegions-Released-20151008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="390" data-file-width="985" decoding="async" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/NH-Pluto-WaterIceDetected-BlueRegions-Released-20151008.jpg/220px-NH-Pluto-WaterIceDetected-BlueRegions-Released-20151008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/NH-Pluto-WaterIceDetected-BlueRegions-Released-20151008.jpg/330px-NH-Pluto-WaterIceDetected-BlueRegions-Released-20151008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/NH-Pluto-WaterIceDetected-BlueRegions-Released-20151008.jpg/440px-NH-Pluto-WaterIceDetected-BlueRegions-Released-20151008.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NH-Pluto-WaterIceDetected-BlueRegions-Released-20151008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Regions where water ice has been detected (blue regions)</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Pluto" title="Geology of Pluto">Geology of Pluto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Pluto" title="Geography of Pluto">Geography of Pluto</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface">Surface</span></h3>\n<p>The plains on Pluto\'s surface are composed of more than 98 percent <a href="/wiki/Solid_nitrogen" title="Solid nitrogen">nitrogen ice</a>, with traces of methane and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tobias_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-tobias-102">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">Nitrogen</a> and carbon monoxide are most abundant on the anti-Charon face of Pluto (around 180&#176; longitude, where <a href="/wiki/Tombaugh_Regio" title="Tombaugh Regio">Tombaugh Regio</a>\'s western lobe, <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_Planitia" title="Sputnik Planitia">Sputnik Planitia</a>, is located), whereas methane is most abundant near 300&#176; east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_2013_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grundy_2013-103">[94]</a></sup> The mountains are made of water ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drake-natgeo_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-drake-natgeo-104">[95]</a></sup> Pluto\'s surface is quite varied, with large differences in both brightness and color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie_2010_light_curve_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buie_2010_light_curve-105">[96]</a></sup> Pluto is one of the most contrastive bodies in the Solar System, with as much contrast as <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">Iapetus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie_web_map_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buie_web_map-106">[97]</a></sup> The color varies from charcoal black, to dark orange and white.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubble2010_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hubble2010-107">[98]</a></sup> Pluto\'s color is more similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a> with slightly more orange and significantly less red than <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie_2010_surface-maps_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buie_2010_surface-maps-108">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Pluto" title="Geography of Pluto">Notable geographical features</a> include Tombaugh Regio, or the "Heart" (a large bright area on the side opposite Charon), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cthulhu_Regio" title="Cthulhu Regio">Cthulhu Macula</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pluto_System_after_New_Horizons_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pluto_System_after_New_Horizons-6">[4]</a></sup> or the "Whale" (a large dark area on the trailing hemisphere), and the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brass_Knuckles_(Pluto)" title="Brass Knuckles (Pluto)">Brass Knuckles</a>" (a series of equatorial dark areas on the leading hemisphere).\n</p><p>Sputnik Planitia, the western lobe of the "Heart", is a 1,000&#160;km-wide basin of frozen nitrogen and carbon monoxide ices, divided into polygonal cells, which are interpreted as <a href="/wiki/Convection_cell" title="Convection cell">convection cells</a> that carry floating blocks of water ice crust and <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimation</a> pits towards their margins;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lakdawalla-DPS-2016-10-26_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-lakdawalla-DPS-2016-10-26-109">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinnon2016_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKinnon2016-110">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trowbridge2016_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trowbridge2016-111">[102]</a></sup> there are obvious signs of glacial flows both into and out of the basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pluto_updates_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pluto_updates-112">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umurhan2016-01-08_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Umurhan2016-01-08-113">[104]</a></sup> It has no craters that were visible to <i>New Horizons</i>, indicating that its surface is less than 10 million years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marchis2016_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marchis2016-114">[105]</a></sup> Latest studies have shown that the surface has an age of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005180000000000000&#9824;</span>180<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span><span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right;">+90<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span><br/>&#8722;40<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span></span></span></span> years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LPSC2017Buhler_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-LPSC2017Buhler-115">[106]</a></sup>\nThe New Horizons science team summarized initial findings as "Pluto displays a surprisingly wide variety of geological landforms, including those resulting from <a href="/wiki/Glaciology" title="Glaciology">glaciological</a> and surface&#8211;atmosphere interactions as well as impact, <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">tectonic</a>, possible <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">cryovolcanic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mass_wasting" title="Mass wasting">mass-wasting</a> processes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2015_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2015-8">[6]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:974px;max-width:974px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:414px;max-width:414px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA20154-Pluto-MapOfOver1000Craters-20151110.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="471" data-file-width="903" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/PIA20154-Pluto-MapOfOver1000Craters-20151110.jpg/412px-PIA20154-Pluto-MapOfOver1000Craters-20151110.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/PIA20154-Pluto-MapOfOver1000Craters-20151110.jpg/618px-PIA20154-Pluto-MapOfOver1000Craters-20151110.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/PIA20154-Pluto-MapOfOver1000Craters-20151110.jpg/824px-PIA20154-Pluto-MapOfOver1000Craters-20151110.jpg 2x" width="412"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">Distribution of over 1000 craters of all ages on Pluto. The variation in density (with none found in <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_Planitia" title="Sputnik Planitia">Sputnik Planitia</a>) indicates a long history of varying geological activity.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:352px;max-width:352px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto%27s_Sputnik_Planum_geologic_map_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3200" data-file-width="5200" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Pluto%27s_Sputnik_Planum_geologic_map_%28cropped%29.jpg/350px-Pluto%27s_Sputnik_Planum_geologic_map_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Pluto%27s_Sputnik_Planum_geologic_map_%28cropped%29.jpg/525px-Pluto%27s_Sputnik_Planum_geologic_map_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Pluto%27s_Sputnik_Planum_geologic_map_%28cropped%29.jpg/700px-Pluto%27s_Sputnik_Planum_geologic_map_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">Geologic map of Sputnik Planitia and surroundings (<a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Pluto%27s_Sputnik_Planum_geologic_map_-_context.jpg" title="c:File:Pluto\'s Sputnik Planum geologic map - context.jpg">context</a>), with <a href="/wiki/Convection_cell" title="Convection cell">convection cell</a> margins outlined in black</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto%E2%80%99s_Heart_-_Like_a_Cosmic_Lava_Lamp.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2944" data-file-width="2736" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Pluto%E2%80%99s_Heart_-_Like_a_Cosmic_Lava_Lamp.jpg/200px-Pluto%E2%80%99s_Heart_-_Like_a_Cosmic_Lava_Lamp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Pluto%E2%80%99s_Heart_-_Like_a_Cosmic_Lava_Lamp.jpg/300px-Pluto%E2%80%99s_Heart_-_Like_a_Cosmic_Lava_Lamp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Pluto%E2%80%99s_Heart_-_Like_a_Cosmic_Lava_Lamp.jpg/400px-Pluto%E2%80%99s_Heart_-_Like_a_Cosmic_Lava_Lamp.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">Sputnik Planitia is covered with churning nitrogen ice "cells" that are geologically young and turning over due to <a href="/wiki/Convection_cell" title="Convection cell">convection</a>.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div><p> In Western parts of Sputnik Planitia there are fields of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transverse_dunes" title="Transverse dunes">transverse dunes</a> formed by the winds blowing from the center of Sputnik Planitia in the direction of surrounding mountains. The dune wavelengths are in the range of 0.4&#8211;1&#160;km and they are likely consists of methane particles 200&#8211;300&#160;&#956;m in size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2018_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2018-116">[107]</a></sup>\n</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Internal_Structure_of_Pluto.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1238" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Internal_Structure_of_Pluto.jpg/220px-Internal_Structure_of_Pluto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Internal_Structure_of_Pluto.jpg/330px-Internal_Structure_of_Pluto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Internal_Structure_of_Pluto.jpg/440px-Internal_Structure_of_Pluto.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Internal_Structure_of_Pluto.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Internal structure of Pluto<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann2006_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hussmann2006-117">[108]</a></sup><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><b>1.</b> Frozen nitrogen<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tobias_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-tobias-102">[93]</a></sup></li><li><b>2.</b> Water ice</li><li><b>3.</b> Rock</li></ul></div></div></div></div>\n<p>Pluto\'s density is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003186000000000000&#9824;</span>1.860<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.013&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2015_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2015-8">[6]</a></sup> Because the decay of radioactive elements would eventually heat the ices enough for the rock to separate from them, scientists expect that Pluto\'s internal structure is differentiated, with the rocky material having settled into a dense <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_(geology)" title="Core (geology)">core</a> surrounded by a <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> of water ice. The diameter of the core is hypothesized to be approximately <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006170000000000000&#9824;</span>1700&#160;km</span>, 70% of Pluto\'s diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann2006_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hussmann2006-117">[108]</a></sup> It is possible that such heating continues today, creating a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsurface_ocean" title="Subsurface ocean">subsurface ocean</a> of liquid water <span class="nowrap">100 to 180 km</span> thick at the core&#8211;mantle boundary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann2006_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hussmann2006-117">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pluto.jhuapl_Inside_Story_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-pluto.jhuapl_Inside_Story-118">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sci_Am_2017_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sci_Am_2017-119">[110]</a></sup> In September 2016, scientists at <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">Brown University</a> simulated the impact thought to have formed <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_Planitia" title="Sputnik Planitia">Sputnik Planitia</a>, and showed that it might have been the result of liquid water upwelling from below after the collision, implying the existence of a subsurface ocean at least 100&#160;km deep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[111]</a></sup> Pluto has no magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[112]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass_and_size">Mass and size</span></h2>\n<table class="wikitable floatright" style="margin-left: 12px; text-align: center;">\n<caption>Selected size estimates for Pluto\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th scope="col">Year\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Radius\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Notes\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">1993\n</th>\n<td>1195&#160;km\n</td>\n<td>Millis, et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Millis_10.1006/icar.1993.1126_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Millis_10.1006/icar.1993.1126-122">[113]</a></sup> (if no haze)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plutosize_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plutosize-123">[114]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">1993\n</th>\n<td>1180&#160;km\n</td>\n<td>Millis, et al. (surface &amp; haze)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plutosize_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plutosize-123">[114]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">1994\n</th>\n<td>1164&#160;km\n</td>\n<td>Young &amp; Binzel<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YoungBinzel_10.1006/icar.1994.1056_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-YoungBinzel_10.1006/icar.1994.1056-124">[115]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">2006\n</th>\n<td>1153&#160;km\n</td>\n<td>Buie, et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuieGrundyYoung_2006_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-BuieGrundyYoung_2006-49">[41]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">2007\n</th>\n<td>1161&#160;km\n</td>\n<td>Young, Young, &amp;
