Buie<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young2007_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Young2007-125">[116]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">2011\n</th>\n<td>1180&#160;km\n</td>\n<td>Zalucha, et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zalucha2011_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zalucha2011-126">[117]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">2014\n</th>\n<td>1184&#160;km\n</td>\n<td>Lellouch, et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lellouch_2015_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lellouch_2015-127">[118]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">2015\n</th>\n<td>1187&#160;km\n</td>\n<td><i>New Horizons</i> measurement (from optical data)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHPC_20150724_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHPC_20150724-128">[119]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">2017\n</th>\n<td>1188.3&#160;km\n</td>\n<td><i>New Horizons</i> measurement (from radio occultation data)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimmo2017_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nimmo2017-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pluto_System_after_New_Horizons_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pluto_System_after_New_Horizons-6">[4]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Pluto\'s diameter is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006237660000000000&#9824;</span>2<span style="margin-left:.25em;">376</span>.6<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>3.2&#160;km</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimmo2017_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nimmo2017-7">[5]</a></sup> and its mass is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7022130299999999999&#9824;</span>(1.303<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.003)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>22</sup>&#160;kg</span>, 17.7% that of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> (0.22% that of Earth).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies2001_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2001-129">[120]</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Surface_area" title="Surface area">surface area</a> is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7013177900000000000&#9824;</span>1.779<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>7</sup>&#160;km<sup>2</sup></span>, or roughly the same surface area as <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Surface_gravity" title="Surface gravity">surface gravity</a> is 0.063 <i>g</i> (compared to 1 <i>g</i> for Earth).\n</p><p>The discovery of Pluto\'s satellite <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a> in 1978 enabled a determination of the mass of the Pluto&#8211;Charon system by application of <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion#Deriving_Kepler\'s_third_law" title="Kepler\'s laws of planetary motion">Newton\'s formulation of Kepler\'s third law</a>. Observations of Pluto in occultation with Charon allowed scientists to establish Pluto\'s diameter more accurately, whereas the invention of <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_optics" title="Adaptive optics">adaptive optics</a> allowed them to determine its shape more accurately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Close_2000_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Close_2000-130">[121]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto,_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2026" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Pluto%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/220px-Pluto%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Pluto%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/330px-Pluto%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Pluto%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/440px-Pluto%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pluto,_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Size comparisons: <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, and Pluto</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><div class="noresize"><map name="ImageMap_2_339231174"><area alt="Ganymede" coords="27,37,33" href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" shape="circle" title="Ganymede"/><area alt="Titan" coords="97,37,32" href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" shape="circle" title="Titan"/><area alt="Callisto" coords="165,35,32" href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" shape="circle" title="Callisto"/><area alt="Io" coords="23,106,24" href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" shape="circle" title="Io"/><area alt="Moon" coords="78,108,22" href="/wiki/Moon" shape="circle" title="Moon"/><area alt="Europa" coords="132,108,20" href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" shape="circle" title="Europa"/><area alt="Triton" coords="180,89,16" href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" shape="circle" title="Triton"/><area alt="Pluto" coords="181,126,14" href="/wiki/Pluto" shape="circle" title="Pluto"/><area alt="File:Pluto compared2.jpg" coords="0,0,195,151" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_compared2.jpg" shape="rect" title="File:Pluto compared2.jpg"/></map><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1341" decoding="async" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Pluto_compared2.jpg/400px-Pluto_compared2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Pluto_compared2.jpg/600px-Pluto_compared2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Pluto_compared2.jpg/800px-Pluto_compared2.jpg 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_2_339231174" width="400"/></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_compared2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pluto (bottom right) compared in size to the largest satellites in the solar system (from left to right and top to bottom): Ganymede, Titan, Callisto, Io, the Moon, Europa, and Triton</div></div></div>\n<p>With less than 0.2 lunar masses, Pluto is much less massive than the <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a>, and also less massive than seven <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>: <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, and Triton. The mass is much less than thought before Charon was discovered.\n</p><p>Pluto is more than twice the diameter and a dozen times the mass of the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>, the largest object in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>. It is less massive than the dwarf planet <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian object</a> discovered in 2005, though Pluto has a larger diameter of 2376.6&#160;km<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimmo2017_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nimmo2017-7">[5]</a></sup> compared to Eris\'s approximate diameter of 2326&#160;km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewHorizons_PlutoSize_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewHorizons_PlutoSize-131">[122]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Determinations of Pluto\'s size had been complicated by its atmosphere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young2007_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Young2007-125">[116]</a></sup> and hydrocarbon haze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plutosize_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-Plutosize-123">[114]</a></sup> In March 2014, Lellouch, de Bergh et al. published findings regarding methane mixing ratios in Pluto\'s atmosphere consistent with a Plutonian diameter greater than 2360&#160;km, with a "best guess" of 2368&#160;km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lellouch_2015_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lellouch_2015-127">[118]</a></sup> On July 13, 2015, images from NASA\'s <i>New Horizons</i> mission Long Range Reconnaissance Imager (LORRI), along with data from the other instruments, determined Pluto\'s diameter to be 2,370&#160;km (1,470&#160;mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewHorizons_PlutoSize_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-NewHorizons_PlutoSize-131">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emily_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-emily-132">[123]</a></sup> which was later revised to be 2,372&#160;km (1,474&#160;mi) on July 24,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHPC_20150724_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-NHPC_20150724-128">[119]</a></sup> and later to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006237400000000000&#9824;</span>2374<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>8&#160;km</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2015_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2015-8">[6]</a></sup> Using radio occultation data from the <i>New Horizons</i> Radio Science Experiment (REX), the diameter was found to be <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006237660000000000&#9824;</span>2<span style="margin-left:.25em;">376</span>.6<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>3.2&#160;km</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimmo2017_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nimmo2017-7">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Pluto" title="Atmosphere of Pluto">Atmosphere of Pluto</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA21590_%E2%80%93_Blue_Rays,_New_Horizons%27_High-Res_Farewell_to_Pluto.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="3200" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/PIA21590_%E2%80%93_Blue_Rays%2C_New_Horizons%27_High-Res_Farewell_to_Pluto.jpg/280px-PIA21590_%E2%80%93_Blue_Rays%2C_New_Horizons%27_High-Res_Farewell_to_Pluto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/PIA21590_%E2%80%93_Blue_Rays%2C_New_Horizons%27_High-Res_Farewell_to_Pluto.jpg/420px-PIA21590_%E2%80%93_Blue_Rays%2C_New_Horizons%27_High-Res_Farewell_to_Pluto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/PIA21590_%E2%80%93_Blue_Rays%2C_New_Horizons%27_High-Res_Farewell_to_Pluto.jpg/560px-PIA21590_%E2%80%93_Blue_Rays%2C_New_Horizons%27_High-Res_Farewell_to_Pluto.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA21590_%E2%80%93_Blue_Rays,_New_Horizons%27_High-Res_Farewell_to_Pluto.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A near-true-color image taken by <i>New Horizons</i> after its flyby. Numerous layers of blue haze float in Pluto\'s atmosphere. Along and near the limb, mountains and their shadows are visible.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA21061-Pluto-DwarfPlanet-XRays-20160914.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3658" data-file-width="5333" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/PIA21061-Pluto-DwarfPlanet-XRays-20160914.jpg/280px-PIA21061-Pluto-DwarfPlanet-XRays-20160914.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/PIA21061-Pluto-DwarfPlanet-XRays-20160914.jpg/420px-PIA21061-Pluto-DwarfPlanet-XRays-20160914.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/PIA21061-Pluto-DwarfPlanet-XRays-20160914.jpg/560px-PIA21061-Pluto-DwarfPlanet-XRays-20160914.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA21061-Pluto-DwarfPlanet-XRays-20160914.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Image of Pluto in X-rays by <a href="/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-ray Observatory">Chandra X-ray Observatory</a> (blue spot). The X-rays are probably created by interaction of the gases surrounding Pluto with solar wind, although details of their origin are not clear.</div></div></div>\n<p>Pluto has a tenuous <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> consisting of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> (N<sub>2</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (CH<sub>4</sub>), and carbon monoxide (CO), which are in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equilibrium_vapor_pressure" title="Equilibrium vapor pressure">equilibrium with their ices</a> on Pluto\'s surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150724-ap_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150724-ap-133">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croswell1992_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Croswell1992-134">[125]</a></sup> According to the measurements by <i>New Horizons</i>, the surface pressure is about 1&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">Pa</a> (10&#160;<a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">&#956;bar</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2015_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2015-8">[6]</a></sup> roughly one million to 100,000 times less than Earth\'s atmospheric pressure. It was initially thought that, as Pluto moves away from the Sun, its atmosphere should gradually freeze onto the surface; studies of <i>New Horizons</i> data and ground-based occultations show that Pluto\'s atmospheric density increases, and that it likely remains gaseous throughout Pluto\'s orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olkin_2015_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olkin_2015-135">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skyandtel_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-skyandtel-136">[127]</a></sup> <i>New Horizons</i> observations showed that atmospheric escape of nitrogen to be 10,000 times less than expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skyandtel_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-skyandtel-136">[127]</a></sup> Alan Stern has contended that even a small increase in Pluto\'s surface temperature can lead to exponential increases in Pluto\'s atmospheric density; from 18&#160;hPa to as much as 280&#160;hPa (three times that of Mars to a quarter that of the Earth). At such densities, nitrogen could flow across the surface as liquid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skyandtel_136-2"><a href="#cite_note-skyandtel-136">[127]</a></sup> Just like sweat cools the body as it evaporates from the skin, the <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimation</a> of Pluto\'s atmosphere cools its surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KerThan2006-CNN_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-KerThan2006-CNN-137">[128]</a></sup> The presence of atmospheric gases was traced up to 1670 kilometers high; the atmosphere does not have a sharp upper boundary.\n</p><p>The presence of methane, a powerful <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a>, in Pluto\'s atmosphere creates a <a href="/wiki/Inversion_(meteorology)" title="Inversion (meteorology)">temperature inversion</a>, with the average temperature of its atmosphere tens of degrees warmer than its surface,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lellouch_2009_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lellouch_2009-138">[129]</a></sup> though observations by <i>New Horizons</i> have revealed Pluto\'s upper atmosphere to be far colder than expected (70&#160;K, as opposed to about 100&#160;K).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skyandtel_136-3"><a href="#cite_note-skyandtel-136">[127]</a></sup> Pluto\'s atmosphere is divided into roughly 20 regularly spaced haze layers  up to 150&#160;km high,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2015_8-8"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2015-8">[6]</a></sup> thought to be the result of pressure waves created by airflow across Pluto\'s mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skyandtel_136-4"><a href="#cite_note-skyandtel-136">[127]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Satellites">Satellites</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Pluto" title="Moons of Pluto">Moons of Pluto</a></div>\n<p>Pluto has five known <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellites</a>. The closest to Pluto is <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>. First identified in 1978 by astronomer <a href="/wiki/James_W._Christy" title="James W. Christy">James Christy</a>, Charon is the only moon of Pluto in <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a>; Charon\'s mass is sufficient to cause the barycenter of the Pluto&#8211;Charon system to be outside Pluto. Beyond Charon there are four much smaller <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circumbinary" title="Circumbinary">circumbinary</a> moons. In order of distance from Pluto they are Styx, Nix, Kerberos, and Hydra. <a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">Nix</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">Hydra</a> were both discovered in 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gugliotta2005_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gugliotta2005-139">[130]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(moon)" title="Kerberos (moon)">Kerberos</a> was discovered in 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P4_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-P4-140">[131]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Styx_(moon)" title="Styx (moon)">Styx</a> was discovered in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[132]</a></sup> The satellites\' orbits are circular (eccentricity &lt; 0.006) and coplanar with Pluto\'s equator (inclination &lt; 1&#176;),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie2012_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buie2012-142">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShowalterHamilton2015_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShowalterHamilton2015-143">[134]</a></sup> and therefore tilted approximately 120&#176; relative to Pluto\'s orbit. The Plutonian system is highly compact: the five known satellites orbit within the inner 3% of the region where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prograde_orbit" title="Prograde orbit">prograde orbits</a> would be stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sternetal_2005_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sternetal_2005-144">[135]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The orbital periods of all Pluto\'s moons are linked in a system of <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonances</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance#Coincidental_\'near\'_ratios_of_mean_motion" title="Orbital resonance">near resonances</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShowalterHamilton2015_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-ShowalterHamilton2015-143">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witze2015_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Witze2015-145">[136]</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Apsidal_precession" title="Apsidal precession">precession</a> is accounted for, the orbital periods of Styx, Nix, and Hydra are in an exact 18:22:33 ratio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShowalterHamilton2015_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-ShowalterHamilton2015-143">[134]</a></sup> There is a sequence of approximate ratios, 3:4:5:6, between the periods of Styx, Nix, Kerberos, and Hydra with that of Charon; the ratios become closer to being exact the further out the moons are.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShowalterHamilton2015_143-3"><a href="#cite_note-ShowalterHamilton2015-143">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matson_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matson-146">[137]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif/220px-Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif/330px-Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif/440px-Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An oblique view of the Pluto&#8211;Charon system showing that Pluto orbits a point outside itself. The two bodies are mutually <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">tidally locked</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Pluto&#8211;Charon system is one of the few in the Solar System whose barycenter lies outside the primary body; the <a href="/wiki/617_Patroclus" title="617 Patroclus">Patroclus&#8211;Menoetius</a> system is a smaller example, and the <a href="/wiki/Jupiter#Mass_and_size" title="Jupiter">Sun&#8211;Jupiter</a> system is the only larger one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RichardsonWalsh2005_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-RichardsonWalsh2005-147">[138]</a></sup> The similarity in size of Charon and Pluto has prompted some astronomers to call it a <a href="/wiki/Double_planet" title="Double planet">double dwarf planet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sicardyetal2006nature_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sicardyetal2006nature-148">[139]</a></sup> The system is also unusual among planetary systems in that each is <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">tidally locked</a> to the other, which means that Pluto and Charon always have the same hemisphere facing each other. From any position on either body, the other is always at the same position in the sky, or always obscured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young1997_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Young1997-149">[140]</a></sup> This also means that the rotation period of each is equal to the time it takes the entire system to rotate around its barycenter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-axis_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-axis-98">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2007, observations by the <a href="/wiki/Gemini_Observatory" title="Gemini Observatory">Gemini Observatory</a> of patches of ammonia hydrates and water crystals on the surface of Charon suggested the presence of active cryo-geysers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spaceflightnow2007_Ice_machine_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-spaceflightnow2007_Ice_machine-150">[141]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pluto\'s moons are hypothesized to have been formed by a collision between Pluto and a similar-sized body, early in the history of the Solar System. The collision released material that consolidated into the moons around Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov-151">[142]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:772px;max-width:772px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:184px;max-width:184px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_moon_P5_discovery_with_moons%27_orbits.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1578" data-file-width="1546" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Pluto_moon_P5_discovery_with_moons%27_orbits.jpg/182px-Pluto_moon_P5_discovery_with_moons%27_orbits.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Pluto_moon_P5_discovery_with_moons%27_orbits.jpg/273px-Pluto_moon_P5_discovery_with_moons%27_orbits.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Pluto_moon_P5_discovery_with_moons%27_orbits.jpg/364px-Pluto_moon_P5_discovery_with_moons%27_orbits.jpg 2x" width="182"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:250px;max-width:250px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png/248px-Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png/372px-Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png/496px-Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png 2x" width="248"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:332px;max-width:332px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nh-pluto_moons_family_portrait-truecolor.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Nh-pluto_moons_family_portrait-truecolor.png/330px-Nh-pluto_moons_family_portrait-truecolor.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Nh-pluto_moons_family_portrait-truecolor.png/495px-Nh-pluto_moons_family_portrait-truecolor.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Nh-pluto_moons_family_portrait-truecolor.png/660px-Nh-pluto_moons_family_portrait-truecolor.png 2x" width="330"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:188px;max-width:188px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charon_in_True_Color_-_High-Res.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Charon_in_True_Color_-_High-Res.jpg/186px-Charon_in_True_Color_-_High-Res.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Charon_in_True_Color_-_High-Res.jpg/279px-Charon_in_True_Color_-_High-Res.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Charon_in_True_Color_-_High-Res.jpg/372px-Charon_in_True_Color_-_High-Res.jpg 2x" width="186"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>1.</b> The Pluto system: Pluto, Charon, <a href="/wiki/Styx_(moon)" title="Styx (moon)">Styx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">Nix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(moon)" title="Kerberos (moon)">Kerberos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">Hydra</a>, imaged by the Hubble Space Telescope in July 2012. <b>2.</b> Pluto and Charon, to scale. Image acquired by <i>New Horizons</i> on July 8, 2015. <b>3.</b> Family portrait of the five moons of Pluto, to scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubblesite.org_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-hubblesite.org-152">[143]</a></sup> <b>4.</b> Pluto\'s moon Charon as viewed by <i>New Horizons</i> on July 13, 2015</div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin"><span id="Origins"></span> Origin</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">Nice model</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Outersolarsystem_objectpositions_labels_comp.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2656" data-file-width="2708" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Outersolarsystem_objectpositions_labels_comp.png/220px-Outersolarsystem_objectpositions_labels_comp.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Outersolarsystem_objectpositions_labels_comp.png/330px-Outersolarsystem_objectpositions_labels_comp.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Outersolarsystem_objectpositions_labels_comp.png/440px-Outersolarsystem_objectpositions_labels_comp.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Outersolarsystem_objectpositions_labels_comp.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plot of the known Kuiper belt objects, set against the four <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Pluto\'s origin and identity had long puzzled astronomers. One early hypothesis was that Pluto was an escaped moon of Neptune,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ley195608_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-ley195608-153">[144]</a></sup> knocked out of orbit by its largest current moon, Triton. This idea was eventually rejected after dynamical studies showed it to be impossible because Pluto never approaches Neptune in its orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[145]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pluto\'s true place in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> began to reveal itself only in 1992, when astronomers began to find small icy objects beyond Neptune that were similar to Pluto not only in orbit but also in size and composition. This trans-Neptunian population is thought to be the source of many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Short-period_comet" title="Short-period comet">short-period comets</a>. Pluto is now known to be the largest member of the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wiki-kbo_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-wiki-kbo-155">[j]</a></sup> a stable belt of objects located between 30 and 50 AU from the Sun. As of 2011, surveys of the Kuiper belt to magnitude 21 were nearly complete and any remaining Pluto-sized objects are expected to be beyond 100&#160;AU from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2011_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2011-156">[146]</a></sup> Like other Kuiper-belt objects (KBOs), Pluto shares features with <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>; for example, the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> is gradually blowing Pluto\'s surface into space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pluto.jhuapl_cousin_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-pluto.jhuapl_cousin-157">[147]</a></sup> It has been claimed that if Pluto were placed as near to the Sun as Earth, it would develop a tail, as comets do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyson1999_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tyson1999-158">[148]</a></sup> This claim has been disputed with the argument that Pluto\'s escape velocity is too high for this to happen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[149]</a></sup> Nonetheless, it has also been claimed that Pluto may have formed as a result of the agglomeration of numerous comets and related Kuiper belt objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SP-20180524_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-SP-20180524-160">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARX-20180524_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARX-20180524-161">[151]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Though Pluto is the largest Kuiper belt object discovered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plutosize_123-3"><a href="#cite_note-Plutosize-123">[114]</a></sup> Neptune\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>, which is slightly larger than Pluto, is similar to it both geologically and atmospherically, and is thought to be a captured Kuiper belt object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlanetaryOrg_Triton_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlanetaryOrg_Triton-162">[152]</a></sup> Eris (<a href="#Classification">see above</a>) is about the same size as Pluto (though more massive) but is not strictly considered a member of the Kuiper belt population. Rather, it is considered a member of a linked population called the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a>.\n</p><p>A large number of Kuiper belt objects, like Pluto, are in a 2:3 orbital resonance with Neptune. KBOs with this orbital resonance are called "<a href="/wiki/Plutino" title="Plutino">plutinos</a>", after Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2004_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2004-163">[153]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Like other members of the Kuiper belt, Pluto is thought to be a residual <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimal</a>; a component of the original <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disc" title="Protoplanetary disc">protoplanetary disc</a> around the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> that failed to fully coalesce into a full-fledged planet. Most astronomers agree that Pluto owes its current position to a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_migration" title="Planetary migration">sudden migration</a> undergone by Neptune early in the Solar System\'s formation. As Neptune migrated outward, it approached the objects in the proto-Kuiper belt, setting one in orbit around itself (Triton), locking others into resonances, and knocking others into chaotic orbits. The objects in the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a>, a dynamically unstable region overlapping the Kuiper belt, are thought to have been placed in their current positions by interactions with Neptune\'s migrating resonances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hahn2005_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hahn2005-164">[154]</a></sup> A computer model created in 2004 by Alessandro Morbidelli of the <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B4te_d%27Azur_Observatory" title="C&#244;te d\'Azur Observatory">Observatoire de la C&#244;te d\'Azur</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nice" title="Nice">Nice</a> suggested that the migration of Neptune into the Kuiper belt may have been triggered by the formation of a 1:2 resonance between Jupiter and Saturn, which created a gravitational push that propelled both Uranus and Neptune into higher orbits and caused them to switch places, ultimately doubling Neptune\'s distance from the Sun. The resultant expulsion of objects from the proto-Kuiper belt could also explain the <a href="/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment" title="Late Heavy Bombardment">Late Heavy Bombardment</a> 600&#160;million years after the Solar System\'s formation and the origin of the <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">Jupiter trojans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levison2007_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levison2007-165">[155]</a></sup> It is possible that Pluto had a near-circular orbit about 33 AU from the Sun before Neptune\'s migration <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbed</a> it into a resonant capture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malhotra1995_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Malhotra1995-166">[156]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">Nice model</a> requires that there were about a thousand Pluto-sized bodies in the original planetesimal disk, which included Triton and Eris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levison2007_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-Levison2007-165">[155]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation_and_exploration">Observation and exploration</span></h2>\n<p>Pluto\'s distance from Earth makes its in-depth study and <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">exploration</a> difficult. On July 14, 2015, NASA\'s <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> space probe flew through the Pluto system, providing much information about it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20160317-sci_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20160317-sci-167">[157]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation">Observation</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_animiert_200px.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/91/Pluto_animiert_200px.gif" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_animiert_200px.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Computer-generated rotating image of Pluto based on observations by the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> in 2002&#8211;2003</div></div></div>\n<p>Pluto\'s visual <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> averages 15.1, brightening to 13.65 at perihelion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-11"><a href="#cite_note-Pluto_Fact_Sheet-2">[1]</a></sup> To see it, a telescope is required; around 30&#160;cm (12&#160;in) aperture being desirable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SSC2002_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-SSC2002-168">[158]</a></sup> It looks star-like and without a visible disk even in large telescopes, because its <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">angular diameter</a> is only 0.11".\n</p><p>The earliest maps of Pluto, made in the late 1980s, were brightness maps created from close observations of eclipses by its largest moon, Charon. Observations were made of the change in the total average brightness of the Pluto&#8211;Charon system during the eclipses. For example, eclipsing a bright spot on Pluto makes a bigger total brightness change than eclipsing a dark spot. Computer processing of many such observations can be used to create a brightness map. This method can also track changes in brightness over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YoungBinzelCrane2001_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-YoungBinzelCrane2001-169">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuieTholenHorne1992_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-BuieTholenHorne1992-170">[160]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Better maps were produced from images taken by the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> (HST), which offered higher <a href="/wiki/Angular_resolution" title="Angular resolution">resolution</a>, and showed considerably more detail,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie_web_map_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buie_web_map-106">[97]</a></sup> resolving variations several hundred kilometers across, including polar regions and large bright spots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie_2010_surface-maps_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buie_2010_surface-maps-108">[99]</a></sup> These maps were produced by complex computer processing, which finds the best-fit projected maps for the few pixels of the Hubble images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie_mapmaking_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buie_mapmaking-171">[161]</a></sup> These remained the most detailed maps of Pluto until the flyby of <i>New Horizons</i> in July 2015, because the two cameras on the HST used for these maps were no longer in service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie_mapmaking_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buie_mapmaking-171">[161]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Pluto" title="Exploration of Pluto">Exploration of Pluto</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto-Map-Annotated.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="3600" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Pluto-Map-Annotated.jpg/220px-Pluto-Map-Annotated.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Pluto-Map-Annotated.jpg/330px-Pluto-Map-Annotated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Pluto-Map-Annotated.jpg/440px-Pluto-Map-Annotated.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pluto-Map-Annotated.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The portions of Pluto\'s surface mapped by <i>New Horizons</i> (annotated)</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i>New Horizons</i> spacecraft, which <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">flew by</a> Pluto in July 2015, is the first and so far only attempt to explore Pluto directly. Launched in 2006, it captured its first (distant) images of Pluto in late September 2006 during a test of the Long Range Reconnaissance Imager.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pluto.jhuapl_First_Pluto_Sighting_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-pluto.jhuapl_First_Pluto_Sighting-172">[162]</a></sup> The images, taken from a distance of approximately 4.2 billion kilometers, confirmed the spacecraft\'s ability to track distant targets, critical for maneuvering toward Pluto and other Kuiper belt objects. In early 2007 the craft made use of a <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a> from <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>.\n</p><p><i>New Horizons</i> made its closest approach to Pluto on July 14, 2015, after a 3,462-day journey across the Solar System. Scientific observations of Pluto began five months before the closest approach and continued for at least a month after the encounter. Observations were conducted using a <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remote sensing</a> package that included <a href="/wiki/Digital_imaging" title="Digital imaging">imaging</a> instruments and a radio science investigation tool, as well as <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopic</a> and other experiments. The scientific goals of <i>New Horizons</i> were to characterize the global geology and morphology of Pluto and its moon Charon, map their surface composition, and analyze Pluto\'s neutral atmosphere and its escape rate.  On October 25, 2016, at 05:48 pm ET, the last bit of data (of a total of 50 billion bits of data; or 6.25 gigabytes) was received from <i>New Horizons</i> from its close encounter with Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161028_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161028-173">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150115(b)_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150115(b)-175">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[166]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the <i>New Horizons</i> flyby, scientists have advocated for an orbiter mission that would return to Pluto to fulfill new science objectives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[167]</a></sup> They include mapping the surface at 30 feet per pixel, observations of Pluto\'s smaller satellites, observations of how Pluto changes as it rotates on its axis, and topographic mapping of Pluto\'s regions that are covered in long-term darkness due to its axial tilt. The last objective could be accomplished using laser pulses to generate a complete topographic map of Pluto. <i>New Horizons</i> principal investigator Alan Stern has advocated for a <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens"><i>Cassini</i></a>-style orbiter that would launch around 2030 (the 100th anniversary of Pluto\'s discovery) and use Charon\'s gravity to adjust its orbit as needed to fulfill science objectives after arriving at the Pluto system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[168]</a></sup> The orbiter could then use Charon\'s gravity to leave the Pluto system and study more KBOs after all Pluto science objectives are completed. A conceptual study funded by the NASA Innovative Advanced Concepts (<a href="/wiki/NASA_Institute_for_Advanced_Concepts" title="NASA Institute for Advanced Concepts">NIAC</a>) program describes a fusion-enabled Pluto orbiter and lander based on the <a href="/wiki/Princeton_field-reversed_configuration_experiment" title="Princeton field-reversed configuration experiment">Princeton field-reversed configuration reactor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PSS_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-PSS-180">[170]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Videos">Videos</span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:762px;max-width:762px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Pluto flyover animated (July 14, 2015)</div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:250px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Pluto-FlyoverAnimation-20150918.webm/854px--Pluto-FlyoverAnimation-20150918.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="29.817" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Pluto-FlyoverAnimation-20150918.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/8e/Pluto-FlyoverAnimation-20150918.webm/Pluto-FlyoverAnimation-20150918.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; (No Audio &#8211; 1080p 60fps)</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="5600" data-file-width="4500" decoding="async" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Solar_system.jpg/22px-Solar_system.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Solar_system.jpg/34px-Solar_system.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Solar_system.jpg/45px-Solar_system.jpg 2x" width="22"/></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Solar_System" title="Portal:Solar System">Solar System portal</a></span></li></ul></div>\n<table class="metadata mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="30" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/30px-Office-book.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/45px-Office-book.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/60px-Office-book.svg.png 2x" width="30"/></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><ul style="font-weight: bold">\n<li><a href="/wiki/Book:Dwarf_Planets_of_the_Solar_System_%26_Their_Satellites" title="Book:Dwarf Planets of the Solar System &amp; Their Satellites">Book: Dwarf Planets of the Solar System &amp; Their Satellites</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Book:Solar_System" title="Book:Solar System">Book: Solar System</a></li>\n</ul></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/How_I_Killed_Pluto_and_Why_It_Had_It_Coming" title="How I Killed Pluto and Why It Had It Coming">How I Killed Pluto and Why It Had It Coming</a></i></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Planets_in_astrology#Pluto" title="Planets in astrology">Pluto in astrology</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Pluto_in_fiction" title="Pluto in fiction">Pluto in fiction</a></li></ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-caption-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-caption_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This photograph was taken by the <a href="/wiki/Ralph_(New_Horizons)" title="Ralph (New Horizons)">Ralph</a> telescope aboard <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> on July 14, 2015 from a distance of 35,445&#160;km (22,025&#160;mi). The most prominent feature in the image, the bright, youthful plains of <a href="/wiki/Tombaugh_Regio" title="Tombaugh Regio">Tombaugh Regio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_Planitia" title="Sputnik Planitia">Sputnik Planitia</a>, can be seen at right. It contrasts the darker, more cratered terrain of <a href="/wiki/Cthulhu_Macula" title="Cthulhu Macula">Cthulhu Macula</a> at lower left. Because of Pluto\'s 119.591&#176; tilt at its axis, the southern hemisphere is barely visible in this image; the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> runs through Cthulhu Macula and the southern parts of Sputnik Planitia.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-MeanElements-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-MeanElements_5-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The mean elements here are from the Theory of the Outer Planets (TOP2013) solution by the Institut de m&#233;canique c&#233;leste et de calcul des &#233;ph&#233;m&#233;rides (IMCCE).  They refer to the standard equinox J2000, the barycenter of the Solar System, and the epoch J2000.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Surface_area-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Surface_area_9-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Surface area derived from the radius <i>r</i>: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle 4\\pi r^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mn>4</mn>\n<mi>&#960;<!-- &#960; --></mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle 4\\pi r^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="4\\pi r^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b81fcce302776a01dc66fc186a1ce0a616b4d772" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:4.597ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span><b>.</b></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Volume-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Volume_10-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Volume <i>v</i> derived from the radius <i>r</i>: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle 4\\pi r^{3}/3}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mn>4</mn>\n<mi>&#960;<!-- &#960; --></mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle 4\\pi r^{3}/3}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="4\\pi r^{3}/3" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a8f404b3cbe747937404f1a0bc0fbc167e6ecb2a" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.922ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span><b>.</b></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Surface_gravity-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Surface_gravity_11-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Surface gravity derived from the mass <i>M</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">gravitational constant</a> <i>G</i> and the radius <i>r</i>: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle GM/r^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>G</mi>\n<mi>M</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle GM/r^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="GM/r^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/40feb34059c7ff32dd89cc344d70cb5dbf617b74" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.534ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Escape_velocity-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Escape_velocity_12-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Escape velocity derived from the mass <i>M</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">gravitational constant</a> <i>G</i> and the radius <i>r</i>: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\sqrt {2GM/r}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>G</mi>\n<mi>M</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>r</mi>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\sqrt {2GM/r}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\sqrt {2GM/r}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cbdfe8b9b99081000866588420a8b868d28fc9d9" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:9.966ex; height:4.843ex;"/></span>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Angular_size-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Angular_size_17-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Based on geometry of minimum and maximum distance from Earth and Pluto radius in the factsheet</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The equivalence is less close in languages whose <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a> differs widely from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_phonology" title="Ancient Greek phonology">Greek\'s</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali</a> <i>Buluuto</i> and <a href="/wiki/Navajo_language" title="Navajo language">Navajo</a> <i>T&#322;&#243;otoo</i>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Perihelion-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Perihelion_83-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The discovery of Charon in 1978 allowed astronomers to accurately calculate the mass of the Plutonian system. But it did not indicate the two bodies\' individual masses, which could only be estimated after other moons of Pluto were discovered in late 2005. As a result, because Pluto came to perihelion in 1989, most Pluto perihelion date estimates are based on the Pluto&#8211;Charon <a href="/wiki/Barycenter" title="Barycenter">barycenter</a>. Charon came to perihelion <a class="external text" href="http://ssd.jpl.nasa.gov/horizons.cgi?find_body=1&amp;body_group=mb&amp;sstr=901" rel="nofollow">4 September 1989.</a> The Pluto&#8211;Charon barycenter came to perihelion <a class="external text" href="http://ssd.jpl.nasa.gov/horizons.cgi?find_body=1&amp;body_group=mb&amp;sstr=9" rel="nofollow">5 September 1989.</a> Pluto came to perihelion <a class="external text" href="http://ssd.jpl.nasa.gov/horizons.cgi?find_body=1&amp;body_group=mb&amp;sstr=999" rel="nofollow">8 September 1989.</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-wiki-kbo-155"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-wiki-kbo_155-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> The dwarf planet <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a> is roughly the same size as Pluto, about 2330&#160;km; Eris is 28% more massive than Pluto. Eris is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scattered-disc_object" title="Scattered-disc object">scattered-disc object</a>, often considered a distinct population from Kuiper-belt objects like Pluto; Pluto is the largest body in the Kuiper belt proper, which excludes the scattered-disc objects.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-Pluto_Fact_Sheet-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-8"><sup><i><b>i</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-9"><sup><i><b>j</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-10"><sup><i><b>k</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pluto_Fact_Sheet_2-11"><sup><i><b>l</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Williams, David R.
